</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Third_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Third Assessment Report">third assessment report (TAR)</a> prominently featured<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> a graph labeled "Millennial Northern Hemisphere temperature reconstruction" based on a 1999 paper by <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Mann" title="Michael E. Mann">Michael E. Mann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Raymond_S._Bradley" title="Raymond S. Bradley">Raymond S. Bradley</a> and Malcolm K. Hughes (MBH99), which has been referred to as the "<a href="/wiki/Hockey_stick_graph" title="Hockey stick graph">hockey stick graph</a>". This graph extended the similar graph in <a href="/w/index.php?title=File:IPCC_1996_SAR_Figure_3.20.png&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="File:IPCC 1996 SAR Figure 3.20.png (page does not exist)">Figure 3.20</a> from the <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Second_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Second Assessment Report">IPCC Second Assessment Report</a> of 1995, and differed from a schematic in the <a href="/wiki/MWP_and_LIA_in_IPCC_reports" class="mw-redirect" title="MWP and LIA in IPCC reports">first assessment report</a> that lacked temperature units, but appeared to depict larger global temperature variations over the past 1000 years, and higher temperatures during the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a> than the mid 20th century. The schematic was not an actual plot of data, and was based on a diagram of temperatures in central England, with temperatures increased on the basis of documentary evidence of Medieval <a href="/wiki/Wine_from_the_United_Kingdom" title="Wine from the United Kingdom">vineyards in England</a>. Even with this increase, the maximum it showed for the Medieval Warm Period did not reach temperatures recorded in central England in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones_09_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_09-115">[114]</a></sup> The MBH99 finding was supported by cited reconstructions by <a href="#CITEREFJonesBriffaBarnettTett1998">Jones et al. 1998</a>, <a href="#CITEREFPollackHuangShen1998">Pollack, Huang &amp; Shen 1998</a>, <a href="#CITEREFCrowleyLowery2000">Crowley &amp; Lowery 2000</a> and <a href="#CITEREFBriffa2000">Briffa 2000</a>, using differing data and methods. The Jones et al. and Briffa reconstructions were overlaid with the MBH99 reconstruction in Figure 2.21 of the IPCC report.<sup id="cite_ref-TAR_2.3.2.2_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TAR_2.3.2.2-117">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>These studies were widely presented as demonstrating that the current warming period is exceptional in comparison to temperatures between 1000 and 1900, and the MBH99 based graph featured in publicity. Even at the draft stage, this finding was disputed by contrarians: in May 2000 <a href="/wiki/Fred_Singer" title="Fred Singer">Fred Singer</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Science_and_Environmental_Policy_Project" class="mw-redirect" title="Science and Environmental Policy Project">Science and Environmental Policy Project</a> held a press event on Capitol Hill, Washington, D.C., featuring comments on the graph <a href="/wiki/Wibj%C3%B6rn_Karl%C3%A9n" title="Wibjörn Karlén">Wibjörn Karlén</a> and Singer argued against the graph at a <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_Commerce,_Science_and_Transportation" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Senate Committee on Commerce, Science and Transportation">United States Senate Committee on Commerce, Science and Transportation</a> hearing on 18 July 2000. Contrarian <a href="/wiki/John_Lawrence_Daly" title="John Lawrence Daly">John Lawrence Daly</a> featured a modified version of the IPCC 1990 schematic, which he mis-identified as appearing in the IPCC 1995 report, and argued that "Overturning its own previous view in the 1995 report, the IPCC presented the 'Hockey Stick' as the new orthodoxy with hardly an apology or explanation for the abrupt U-turn since its 1995 report".<sup id="cite_ref-Daly_01_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daly_01-118">[117]</a></sup> Criticism of the MBH99 reconstruction in a review paper, which was quickly discredited in the <a href="/wiki/Soon_and_Baliunas_controversy" title="Soon and Baliunas controversy">Soon and Baliunas controversy</a>, was picked up by the Bush administration, and a Senate speech by US Republican senator <a href="/wiki/James_Inhofe" class="mw-redirect" title="James Inhofe">James Inhofe</a> alleged that "manmade global warming is the greatest hoax ever perpetrated on the American people". The data and methodology used to produce the "hockey stick graph" was criticized in papers by <a href="/wiki/Stephen_McIntyre" class="mw-redirect" title="Stephen McIntyre">Stephen McIntyre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ross_McKitrick" title="Ross McKitrick">Ross McKitrick</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-MM05a_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MM05a-119">[118]</a></sup> and in turn the criticisms in these papers were examined by other studies and comprehensively refuted by <a href="#CITEREFWahlAmmann2007">Wahl &amp; Ammann 2007</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Weart_fn48_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weart_fn48-120">[119]</a></sup> which showed errors in the methods used by McIntyre and McKitrick.<sup id="cite_ref-ar4-ch6_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ar4-ch6-121">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 23 June 2005, Rep. <a href="/wiki/Joe_Barton" title="Joe Barton">Joe Barton</a>, chairman of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Energy_and_Commerce" title="United States House Committee on Energy and Commerce">House Committee on Energy and Commerce</a> wrote joint letters with <a href="/wiki/Ed_Whitfield" title="Ed Whitfield">Ed Whitfield</a>, chairman of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Energy_Subcommittee_on_Oversight_and_Investigations" title="United States House Energy Subcommittee on Oversight and Investigations">Subcommittee on Oversight and Investigations</a> demanding full records on climate research, as well as personal information about their finances and careers, from Mann, Bradley and Hughes.<sup id="cite_ref-wapo_Witches_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wapo_Witches-122">[121]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sherwood_Boehlert" title="Sherwood Boehlert">Sherwood Boehlert</a>, chairman of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Science,_Space_and_Technology" title="United States House Committee on Science, Space and Technology">House Science Committee</a>, said this was a "misguided and illegitimate investigation" apparently aimed at intimidating scientists, and at his request the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">U.S. National Academy of Sciences</a> arranged for its <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Research Council">National Research Council</a> to set up a special investigation.<sup id="cite_ref-Revkin606_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Revkin606-123">[122]</a></sup> The National Research Council's report agreed that there were some statistical failings, but these had little effect on the graph, which was generally correct. In a 2006 letter to <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>, Mann, Bradley, and Hughes pointed out that their original article had said that "more widespread high-resolution data are needed before more confident conclusions can be reached" and that the uncertainties were "the point of the article".<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fourth Assessment Report">IPCC Fourth Assessment Report</a> (AR4) published in 2007 featured a graph showing 12 proxy based temperature reconstructions, including the three highlighted in the 2001 Third Assessment Report (TAR); <a href="#CITEREFMannBradleyHughes1999">Mann, Bradley &amp; Hughes 1999</a> as before, <a href="#CITEREFJonesBriffaBarnettTett1998">Jones et al. 1998</a> and <a href="#CITEREFBriffa2000">Briffa 2000</a> had both been calibrated by newer studies. In addition, analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a> cited reconstructions by <a href="#CITEREFCrowleyLowery2000">Crowley &amp; Lowery 2000</a> (as cited in the TAR) and <a href="#CITEREFOsbornBriffa2006">Osborn &amp; Briffa 2006</a>. Ten of these 14 reconstructions covered 1,000 years or longer. Most reconstructions shared some data series, particularly tree ring data, but newer reconstructions used additional data and covered a wider area, using a variety of statistical methods. The section discussed the <a href="/wiki/Divergence_problem" title="Divergence problem">divergence problem</a> affecting certain tree ring data.<sup id="cite_ref-AR4_2k_years_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AR4_2k_years-125">[124]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservative_nature_of_IPCC_reports">Conservative nature of IPCC reports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Conservative nature of IPCC reports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some critics have contended that the IPCC reports tend to underestimate dangers, understate risks, and report only the "lowest common denominator" findings.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 1 February 2007, the eve of the publication of IPCC's major report on climate, a study was published suggesting that temperatures and sea levels have been rising at or above the maximum rates proposed during the last IPCC report in 2001.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_1_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_1-127">[126]</a></sup> The study compared IPCC 2001 projections on temperature and sea level change with observations. Over the six years studied, the actual temperature rise was near the top end of the range given by IPCC's 2001 projection, and the actual sea level rise was above the top of the range of the IPCC projection.</p>
<p>Another example of scientific research which suggests that previous estimates by the IPCC, far from overstating dangers and risks, have actually understated them is a study on projected rises in sea levels. When the researchers' analysis was "applied to the possible scenarios outlined by the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC), the researchers found that in 2100 sea levels would be 0.5–1.4 m [50–140 cm] above 1990 levels. These values are much greater than the 9–88&#160;cm as projected by the IPCC itself in its Third Assessment Report, published in 2001". This may have been due, in part, to the expanding human understanding of climate.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_2_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2-128">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Telegraph_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Telegraph-129">[128]</a></sup></p>
<p>In reporting criticism by some scientists that IPCC's then-impending January 2007 report understates certain risks, particularly sea level rises, an AP story quoted <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Rahmstorf" title="Stefan Rahmstorf">Stefan Rahmstorf</a>, professor of physics and oceanography at Potsdam University as saying "In a way, it is one of the strengths of the IPCC to be very conservative and cautious and not overstate any climate change risk".<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his December 2006 book, <i><a href="/wiki/Hell_and_High_Water:_Global_Warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Hell and High Water: Global Warming">Hell and High Water: Global Warming</a></i>, and in an interview on <a href="/wiki/Fox_News" title="Fox News">Fox News</a> on 31 January 2007, energy expert <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Romm" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseph Romm">Joseph Romm</a> noted that the <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fourth Assessment Report">IPCC Fourth Assessment Report</a> is already out of date and omits recent observations and factors contributing to global warming, such as the release of greenhouse gases from thawing tundra.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p>Political influence on the IPCC has been documented by the release of a memo by ExxonMobil to the Bush administration, and its effects on the IPCC's leadership. The memo led to strong Bush administration lobbying, evidently at the behest of ExxonMobil, to oust <a href="/wiki/Robert_Watson_(scientist)" title="Robert Watson (scientist)">Robert Watson</a>, a climate scientist, from the IPCC chairmanship, and to have him replaced by Pachauri, who was seen at the time as more mild-mannered and industry-friendly.<sup id="cite_ref-New_Scientist_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_Scientist-132">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Common_Dreams_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Common_Dreams-133">[132]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="IPCC_processes">IPCC processes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: IPCC processes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Oppenheimer" title="Michael Oppenheimer">Michael Oppenheimer</a>, a long-time participant in the IPCC and coordinating lead author of the Fifth Assessment Report conceded in <a href="/wiki/Science_Magazine" title="Science Magazine">Science Magazine</a>'s State of the Planet 2008–2009 some limitations of the IPCC consensus approach and asks for concurring, smaller assessments of special problems instead of the large scale approach as in the previous IPCC assessment reports.<sup id="cite_ref-MO_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MO-108">[107]</a></sup> It has become more important to provide a broader exploration of uncertainties.<sup id="cite_ref-MO_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MO-108">[107]</a></sup> Others see as well mixed blessings of the drive for consensus within the IPCC process and ask to include dissenting or minority positions<sup id="cite_ref-hu_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hu-134">[133]</a></sup> or to improve statements about uncertainties.<sup id="cite_ref-nocon_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nocon-135">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>The IPCC process on climate change and its efficiency and success has been compared with dealings with other environmental challenges (compare <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion_and_global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Ozone depletion and global warming">Ozone depletion and global warming</a>). In case of the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion" title="Ozone depletion">Ozone depletion</a> global regulation based on the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Protocol" title="Montreal Protocol">Montreal Protocol</a> has been successful, in case of Climate Change, the <a href="/wiki/Kyoto_Protocol" title="Kyoto Protocol">Kyoto Protocol</a> failed.<sup id="cite_ref-cass_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cass-137">[136]</a></sup> The Ozone case was used to assess the efficiency of the IPCC process.<sup id="cite_ref-RG_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RG-138">[137]</a></sup> The lockstep situation of the IPCC is having built a broad science consensus while states and governments still follow different, if not opposing goals.<sup id="cite_ref-CLT_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CLT-139">[138]</a></sup> The underlying linear model of policy-making of <i>more knowledge we have, the better the political response will be</i> is being doubted.<sup id="cite_ref-CLT_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CLT-139">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-par_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-par-140">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Sheldon Ungar's comparison with global warming, the actors in the ozone depletion case had a better understanding of scientific ignorance and uncertainties.<sup id="cite_ref-Ungar_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ungar-141">[140]</a></sup> The ozone case communicated to lay persons "with easy-to-understand bridging metaphors derived from the <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">popular culture</a>" and related to "immediate risks with everyday relevance", while the <a href="/wiki/Public_opinion_on_climate_change" title="Public opinion on climate change">public opinion on climate change</a> sees no imminent danger.<sup id="cite_ref-Ungar_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ungar-141">[140]</a></sup> The stepwise mitigation of the ozone layer challenge was based as well on successfully reducing regional burden sharing conflicts.<sup id="cite_ref-RG_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RG-138">[137]</a></sup> In case of the IPCC conclusions and the failure of the Kyoto Protocol, varying regional cost-benefit analysis and burden-sharing conflicts with regard to the distribution of emission reductions remain an unsolved problem.<sup id="cite_ref-cass_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cass-137">[136]</a></sup> In the UK, a report for a House of Lords committee asked to urge the IPCC to involve better assessments of costs and benefits of climate change<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup> but the <a href="/wiki/Stern_Review" title="Stern Review">Stern Review</a> ordered by the UK government made a stronger argument in favor to combat human-made climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outdatedness_of_reports">Outdatedness of reports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Outdatedness of reports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Since the IPCC does not carry out its own research, it operates on the basis of scientific papers and independently documented results from other scientific bodies, and its schedule for producing reports requires a deadline for submissions prior to the report's final release. In principle, this means that any significant new evidence or events that change our understanding of climate science between this deadline and publication of an IPCC report cannot be included. In an area of science where our scientific understanding is rapidly changing, this has been raised as a serious shortcoming in a body which is widely regarded as the ultimate authority on the science.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup> However, there has generally been a steady evolution of key findings and levels of scientific confidence from one assessment report to the next.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The submission deadlines for the Fourth Assessment Report (AR4) differed for the reports of each Working Group. Deadlines for the Working Group I report were adjusted during the drafting and review process in order to ensure that reviewers had access to unpublished material being cited by the authors. The final deadline for cited publications was 24 July 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup> The final WG I report was released on 30 April 2007 and the final AR4 Synthesis Report was released on 17 November 2007.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rajendra_Pachauri" class="mw-redirect" title="Rajendra Pachauri">Rajendra Pachauri</a>, the IPCC chair, admitted at the launch of this report that since the IPCC began work on it, scientists have recorded "much stronger trends in climate change", like the unforeseen dramatic melting of polar ice in the summer of 2007,<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup> and added, "that means you better start with intervention much earlier".<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Burden_on_participating_scientists">Burden on participating scientists</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Burden on participating scientists">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Scientists who participate in the IPCC assessment process do so without any compensation other than the normal salaries they receive from their home institutions. The process is labor-intensive, diverting time and resources from participating scientists' research programs.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup> Concerns have been raised that the large uncompensated time commitment and disruption to their own research may discourage qualified scientists from participating.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 2010, Pachauri noted that the IPCC currently had no process for responding to errors or flaws once it issued a report. The problem, according to Pachauri, was that once a report was issued the panels of scientists producing the reports were disbanded.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-150">[149]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_organizational_overhaul">Proposed organizational overhaul</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Proposed organizational overhaul">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In February 2010, in response to controversies regarding claims in the <a href="/wiki/AR4" class="mw-redirect" title="AR4">Fourth Assessment Report</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup> five climate scientists – all contributing or lead IPCC report authors – wrote in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i> calling for changes to the IPCC. They suggested a range of new organizational options, from tightening the selection of lead authors and contributors, to dumping it in favor of a small permanent body, or even turning the whole climate science assessment process into a <a href="/wiki/Internet_Forum#Moderators" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet Forum">moderated</a> "living" Wikipedia-IPCC.<sup id="cite_ref-nature_100211_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nature_100211-153">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-theaustralian_overhaul_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theaustralian_overhaul-154">[153]</a></sup> Other recommendations included that the panel employ a full-time staff and remove government oversight from its processes to avoid political interference.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="InterAcademy_Council_review">InterAcademy Council review</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: InterAcademy Council review">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In March 2010, at the invitation of the United Nations secretary-general and the chair of the IPCC, the <a href="/wiki/InterAcademy_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="InterAcademy Council">InterAcademy Council</a> (IAC) was asked to review the IPCC's processes for developing its reports.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup> The IAC panel, chaired by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Tafler_Shapiro" title="Harold Tafler Shapiro">Harold Tafler Shapiro</a>, convened on 14 May 2010 and released its report on 1 September 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-150">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>The IAC found that, "The IPCC assessment process has been successful overall". The panel, however, made seven formal recommendations for improving the IPCC's assessment process, including:</p>
<ol>
<li>establish an executive committee;</li>
<li>elect an executive director whose term would only last for one assessment;</li>
<li>encourage review editors to ensure that all reviewer comments are adequately considered and genuine controversies are adequately reflected in the assessment reports;</li>
<li>adopt a better process for responding to reviewer comments;</li>
<li>working groups should use a qualitative level-of-understanding scale in the Summary for Policy Makers and Technical Summary;</li>
<li>"Quantitative probabilities (as in the likelihood scale) should be used to describe the probability of well-defined outcomes only when there is sufficient evidence"; and</li>
<li>implement a communications plan that emphasizes transparency and establish guidelines for who can speak on behalf of the organization.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>The panel also advised that the IPCC avoid appearing to advocate specific policies in response to its scientific conclusions.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> Commenting on the IAC report, <i>Nature News</i> noted that "The proposals were met with a largely favourable response from climate researchers who are eager to move on after the media scandals and credibility challenges that have rocked the United Nations body during the past nine months".<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Archiving">Archiving</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Archiving">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Papers and electronic files of certain working groups of the IPCC, including reviews and comments on drafts of their Assessment Reports, are archived at the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://hcl.harvard.edu/collections/ipcc/">Environmental Science and Public Policy Archives</a> in the Harvard Library.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Endorsements_of_the_IPCC">Endorsements of the IPCC</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Endorsements of the IPCC">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Various scientific bodies have issued official statements endorsing and concurring with the findings of the IPCC.</p>
<ul>
<li>Joint <a href="/wiki/National_academy" title="National academy">science academies</a>' statement of 2001. "The work of the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) represents the <a href="/wiki/Consensus" class="mw-redirect" title="Consensus">consensus</a> of the international <a href="/wiki/Scientific_community" title="Scientific community">scientific community</a> on climate change science. We recognise IPCC as the world's most reliable source of information on climate change and its causes, and we endorse its method of achieving this consensus".<sup id="cite_ref-2001_science_academies_statement_40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2001_science_academies_statement-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Canadian_Foundation_for_Climate_and_Atmospheric_Sciences" title="Canadian Foundation for Climate and Atmospheric Sciences">Canadian Foundation for Climate and Atmospheric Sciences</a>. "We concur with the climate science assessment of the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC) in 2001 ... We endorse the conclusions of the IPCC assessment..."<sup id="cite_ref-cfcas_tar_endorsement_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cfcas_tar_endorsement-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Canadian_Meteorological_and_Oceanographic_Society" title="Canadian Meteorological and Oceanographic Society">Canadian Meteorological and Oceanographic Society</a>. "CMOS endorses the process of periodic climate science assessment carried out by the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change and supports the conclusion, in its <a href="/wiki/Third_Assessment_Report" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Assessment Report">Third Assessment Report</a>, which states that the balance of evidence suggests a discernible human influence on global climate."<sup id="cite_ref-cmos_tar_endorsement_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cmos_tar_endorsement-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="&quot;the balance of evidence...&quot; statement is from the IPCC Second Assessment Report, not the IPCC Third Assessment Report (April 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/European_Geosciences_Union" title="European Geosciences Union">European Geosciences Union</a>. "The Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change [...] is the main representative of the global scientific community [...][The] IPCC third assessment report [...] represents the state-of-the-art of climate science supported by the major science academies around the world and by the vast majority of scientific researchers and investigations as documented by the peer-reviewed scientific literature".<sup id="cite_ref-egu_tar_endorsement_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-egu_tar_endorsement-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/International_Council_for_Science" title="International Council for Science">International Council for Science</a>. "...the <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fourth Assessment Report">IPCC 4th Assessment Report</a> represents the most comprehensive international scientific assessment ever conducted. This assessment reflects the current collective knowledge on the climate system, its evolution to date, and its anticipated future development".<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a> (USA). "Internationally, the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC)... is the most senior and authoritative body providing scientific advice to global policy makers".<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Research Council">United States National Research Council</a>. "The <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Third_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Third Assessment Report">IPCC Third Assessment Report</a>'] conclusion that most of the observed warming of the last 50 years is likely to have been due to the increase in greenhouse gas concentrations accurately reflects the current thinking of the scientific community on this issue".<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[163]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Network_of_African_Science_Academies" title="Network of African Science Academies">Network of African Science Academies</a>. "The IPCC should be congratulated for the contribution it has made to public understanding of the nexus that exists between energy, climate and sustainability".<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-165">[164]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Royal_Meteorological_Society" title="Royal Meteorological Society">Royal Meteorological Society</a>, in response to the release of the <a href="/wiki/IPCC_Fourth_Assessment_Report" title="IPCC Fourth Assessment Report">Fourth Assessment Report</a>, referred to the IPCC as "The world's best climate scientists".<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup></li>
<li>Stratigraphy Commission of the <a href="/wiki/Geological_Society_of_London" title="Geological Society of London">Geological Society of London</a>. "The most authoritative assessment of climate change in the near future is provided by the Inter-Governmental Panel for Climate Change".<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<tr>
<th scope="row" class="navbox-group">1901–1925</th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">1901</span> <a href="/wiki/Henry_Dunant" title="Henry Dunant">Henry Dunant</a>&#160;/ <a href="/wiki/Fr%C3%A9d%C3%A9ric_Passy" title="Frédéric Passy">Frédéric Passy</a></li>
